[#Alchian1972]: Armen A. Alchian & Harold Demsetz, *Production, Information Costs, and Economic Organization*, 62 Am. Econ. Rev. 777 (1972) (the firm is not a hierarchy but a specialized market for measuring team production) (*"Production Information Costs"*).

[#Berle1932]: Adolf A. Berle, Jr. & Gardiner C. Means, *The Modern Corporation and Private Property* (1932) (foundational thesis on the separation of ownership and control; the managerialist challenge to property rights) (*"Modern Corporation and Private Property"*).

[#Blair1999]: Margaret M. Blair & Lynn A. Stout, *A Team Production Theory of Corporate Law*, 85 Va. L. Rev. 247 (1999) (the economic alternative to agency theory; the board acts as a "mediating hierarch" to coordinate team production among stakeholders) (*"Team Production Theory"*).

[#Cary1974]: William L. Cary, *Federalism and Corporate Law: Reflections Upon Delaware*, 83 Yale L.J. 663 (1974) (arguing Delaware leads a "race to the bottom" in corporate standards to attract franchise taxes) (*"Federalism and Corporate Law"*).

[#Coase1937]: Ronald H. Coase, *The Nature of the Firm*, 4 Economica 386 (1937) (foundational economic theory; firms exist to reduce the transaction costs of using the price mechanism) (*"Nature of the Firm"*).

[#Easterbrook1991]: Frank H. Easterbrook & Daniel R. Fischel, *The Economic Structure of Corporate Law* (1991) (definitive contractarian text; corporate law is a set of default rules to maximize shareholder wealth) (*"Economic Structure of Corporate Law"*).

[#Fama1965]: Eugene F. Fama, *The Behavior of Stock-Market Prices*, 38 J. Bus. 34 (1965) (foundational empirical work; argues stock price movements follow a random walk, supporting the view that successive price changes are independent and unpredictable) (*"Behavior of Stock-Market Prices"*).

[#Fama1970]: Eugene F. Fama, *Efficient Capital Markets: A Review of Theory and Empirical Work*, 25 J. Fin. 383 (1970) (seminal review article establishing the efficient market hypothesis; three forms of efficiency [weak, semi-strong, strong] define the information reflected in prices) (*"Efficient Capital Markets"*).

[#Friedman1970]: Milton Friedman, *The Social Responsibility of Business Is to Increase Its Profits*, N.Y. Times Mag., Sept. 13, 1970 (the "Friedman Doctrine"; foundational normative argument that the only social responsibility of business is to maximize profits within legal bounds) (*"Social Responsibility of Business"*).

[#Hansmann2001]: Henry B. Hansmann & Reinier Kraakman, *The End of History for Corporate Law*, 89 Geo. L.J. 439 (2001) (triumphalist argument that the shareholder-oriented model has defeated all rival governance systems globally) (*"End of History for Corporate Law"*).

[#Hart1990]: Oliver D. Hart & John Moore, *Property Rights and the Nature of the Firm*, 98 J. Pol. Econ. 1119 (1990) (foundational incomplete contract theory; firm boundaries are defined by the allocation of "residual control rights" over assets) (*"Property Rights and the Nature of the Firm"*).

[#Jensen1976]: Michael C. Jensen & William H. Meckling, *Theory of the Firm: Managerial Behavior, Agency Costs and Ownership Structure*, 3 J. Fin. Econ. 305 (1976) (defining the firm as a "nexus of contracts"; introducing agency cost theory) (*"Theory of the Firm"*).

[#Jensen1978]: Michael C. Jensen, *Some Anomalous Evidence Regarding Market Efficiency*, 6 J. Fin. Econ. 95 (1978) (famous defense of EMH; stating "there is no other proposition in economics which has more solid empirical evidence") (*"Anomalous Evidence Regarding Market Efficiency"*).

[#LaPorta2000]: Rafael La Porta, Florencio Lopez-de-Silanes, Andrei Shleifer & Robert W. Vishny, *Investor Protection and Corporate Governance*, 58 J. Fin. Econ. 3 (2000) (seminal "Law and Finance" study; demonstrating that legal origins [Common vs. Civil Law] predict ownership concentration and valuation globally) (*"Investor Protection and Corporate Governance"*).

[#Malkiel1973]: Burton G. Malkiel, *A Random Walk Down Wall Street: The Time-Tested Strategy for Successful Investing* (W.W. Norton & Co. 1973) (popular exposition of efficient market hypothesis and random walk theory; argues that active investors cannot systematically beat passive index investing) (*"Random Walk Down Wall Street"*).

[#Manne1965]: Henry G. Manne, *Mergers and the Market for Corporate Control*, 73 J. Pol. Econ. 110 (1965) (economic defense of takeovers; the market for control is the ultimate disciplinary mechanism for inefficient management) (*"Mergers and the Market for Corporate Control"*).

[#ModiglianiMiller1958]: Franco Modigliani & Merton H. Miller, *The Cost of Capital, Corporation Finance and the Theory of Investment*, 48 Am. Econ. Rev. 261 (1958) (foundational theory of capital structure; the "irrelevance propositions" arguing that corporate value is independent of capital structure) (*"Cost of Capital Corporation Finance"*).

[#Romano1993]: Roberta Romano, *The Genius of American Corporate Law* (1993) (empirical defense of regulatory competition; Delaware's dominance is due to responsiveness and expertise, a "race to the top") (*"Genius of American Corporate Law"*).

[#Winter1977]: Ralph K. Winter, Jr., *State Law, Shareholder Protection, and the Theory of the Corporation*, 6 J. Legal Stud. 251 (1977) (response to Cary; capital markets discipline states, ensuring that "racing" states produce efficient rules) (*"State Law Shareholder Protection"*).


[#Bainbridge2003]: Stephen M. Bainbridge, *Director Primacy: The Means and Ends of Corporate Governance*, 97 Nw. U. L. Rev. 547 (2003) (competing theory to shareholder primacy; argues corporate law vests power in the board, not shareholders, to overcome collective action problems) (*"Director Primacy"*).

[#Bebchuk2009]: Lucian A. Bebchuk, Alma Cohen & Allen Ferrell, *What Matters in Corporate Governance?*, 22 Rev. Fin. Stud. 783 (2009) (introducing the "E-Index"; empirical proof that specific entrenchment provisions [like staggered boards] negatively correlate with firm value) (*"What Matters in Corporate Governance"*).

[#BlackGilson1998]: Bernard S. Black & Ronald J. Gilson, *Venture Capital and the Structure of Capital Markets: Banks Versus Stock Markets*, 47 J. Fin. Econ. 243 (1998) (comparative institutional analysis; arguing that active venture capital markets require robust stock markets [IPO exit] to allow entrepreneurs to reacquire control from VCs) (*"Venture Capital and the Structure of Capital Markets"*).

[#Chen2022]: Vivien Chen, *Corporate Law and Political Economy in a Kleptocracy*, 70 Am. J. Comp. L. 480 (2022) (analyzing how corporate law structures facilitate state looting in non-democratic regimes; the dark side of asset shielding) (*"Corporate Law and Political Economy in a Kleptocracy"*).

[#Gilson2003]: Ronald J. Gilson, *Engineering a Venture Capital Market: Lessons from the American Experience*, 55 Stan. L. Rev. 1067 (2003) (institutional design theory; identifying the "simultaneity problem" in creating VC markets and the critical role of weak non-compete enforcement in Silicon Valley's success) (*"Engineering a Venture Capital Market"*).

[#Horwitz1977]: Morton J. Horwitz, *The Transformation of American Law, 1780-1860* (1977) (critical history; 19th-century judges reshaped common law to subsidize industrial growth at the expense of weaker parties) (*"Transformation of American Law"*).

[#Hurst1970]: James Willard Hurst, *The Legitimacy of the Business Corporation in the Law of the United States* (1970) (historical shift from the corporation as a public "privilege" for utility to a private "right" for profit) (*"Legitimacy of the Business Corporation"*).

[#Millon1991]: David K. Millon, *Redefining Corporate Law*, 24 Ind. L. Rev. 223 (1991) (historical critique arguing corporate law is fundamentally public law; challenging the private/nexus-of-contracts distinction) (*"Redefining Corporate Law"*).

[#Pistor2019]: Katharina Pistor, *The Code of Capital: How the Law Creates Wealth and Inequality* (2019) (institutional analysis; lawyers "code" assets with priority, durability, and convertibility to create wealth and inequality) (*"Code of Capital"*).

[#Roe2003]: Mark J. Roe, *Political Determinants of Corporate Governance: Political Context, Corporate Impact* (2003) (comparative analysis; national politics, specifically the power of labor, determine ownership concentration levels) (*"Political Determinants of Corporate Governance"*).

[#Roe1994]: Mark J. Roe, *Strong Managers, Weak Owners: The Political Roots of American Corporate Finance* (1994) (arguing US dispersed ownership is a result of populist political restrictions on finance, not just economic efficiency) (*"Strong Managers Weak Owners"*).

[#Stout2012]: Lynn A. Stout, *The Shareholder Value Myth: How Putting Shareholders First Harms Investors, Corporations, and the Public* (Berrett-Koehler Publishers 2012) (comprehensive debunking of shareholder primacy; argues U.S. corporate law does not require shareholder value maximization) (*"Shareholder Value Myth"*).


[#Choike2023]: Anne M. Choike, Usha R. Rodrigues & Kelli Alces Williams (eds.), *Feminist Judgments: Corporate Law Rewritten* (2023) (critical project rewriting famous corporate cases like *Revlon* and *Dodge* from feminist perspectives) (*"Feminist Judgments"*).

[#DiMaggio1983]: Paul J. DiMaggio & Walter W. Powell, *The Iron Cage Revisited: Institutional Isomorphism and Collective Rationality in Organizational Fields*, 48 Am. Soc. Rev. 147 (1983) (sociological theory; firms mimic each other due to uncertainty and professional norms, leading to homogeneity) (*"Iron Cage Revisited"*).

[#Fligstein1990]: Neil Fligstein, *The Transformation of Corporate Control* (1990) (historical sociology; the shift in the "conception of control" from manufacturing to sales to the current "finance conception") (*"Transformation of Corporate Control"*).

[#Granovetter1985]: Mark S. Granovetter, *Economic Action and Social Structure: The Problem of Embeddedness*, 91 Am. J. Soc. 481 (1985) (critique of atomized economics; economic action is "embedded" in social networks and personal relations) (*"Economic Action and Social Structure"*).

[#Greenfield2011]: Kent Greenfield, *The Failure of Corporate Law: Fundamental Flaws and Progressive Possibilities* (2011) (progressive critique; arguing that the "public/private" distinction in corporate law improperly externalizes social costs onto stakeholders) (*"Failure of Corporate Law"*).

[#Harris1993]: Cheryl I. Harris, *Whiteness as Property*, 106 Harv. L. Rev. 1707 (1993) (critical race theory; whiteness functions as a property interest that excludes others from corporate and economic hierarchies) (*"Whiteness as Property"*).

[#Kennedy1976]: Duncan Kennedy, *Form and Substance in Private Law Adjudication*, 89 Harv. L. Rev. 1685 (1976) (CLS critique; the conflict between "rules" and "standards" reflects deeper political conflict between individualism and altruism) (*"Form and Substance in Private Law Adjudication"*).

[#Lahey1985]: Kathleen A. Lahey & Sarah W. Salter, *Corporate Law in Legal Theory and Legal Scholarship: From Classicism to Feminism*, 23 Osgoode Hall L.J. 543 (1985) (early feminist critique; the corporate form as a patriarchal construct separating production from reproduction) (*"Corporate Law in Legal Theory and Legal Scholarship"*).

[#Leong2013]: Nancy Leong, *Racial Capitalism*, 126 Harv. L. Rev. 2151 (2013) (analyzing how white institutions derive value from non-white racial identity ["diversity"] without sharing power) (*"Racial Capitalism"*).

[#Macaulay1963]: Stewart Macaulay, *Non-Contractual Relations in Business: A Preliminary Study*, 28 Am. Soc. Rev. 55 (1963) (empirical study; businessmen rely on norms and relationships rather than legal contracts to enforce deals) (*"Non-Contractual Relations in Business"*).

[#Macneil2001]: Ian Roderick Macneil, *The Relational Theory of Contract: Selected Works of Ian Macneil* (David Campbell ed., 2001) (the theoretical companion to Macaulay; contracts are long-term relationships governed by norms of solidarity and reciprocity, not discrete transactions) (*"Relational Theory of Contract"*).

[#Ramirez2023]: Steven A. Ramirez, *A Vision of the Anti-Racist Public Corporation*, 91 U. Cin. L. Rev. 1 (2023) (arguing that current corporate governance structures perpetuate racial inequality and proposing structural reforms) (*"Anti-Racist Public Corporation"*).

[#Robinson1983]: Cedric J. Robinson, *Black Marxism: The Making of the Black Radical Tradition* (1983) (defining "Racial Capitalism"; capitalism inherently requires the differentiation of human value to function) (*"Black Marxism"*).

[#Unger1983]: Roberto Mangabeira Unger, *The Critical Legal Studies Movement*, 96 Harv. L. Rev. 561 (1983) (CLS manifesto; law is "frozen politics" and the public/private distinction is a political choice) (*"Critical Legal Studies Movement"*).


[#BuchananTullock1962]: James M. Buchanan & Gordon Tullock, *The Calculus of Consent: Logical Foundations of Constitutional Democracy* (1962) (foundational text of public choice theory; analyzing political decision-making through economic lens).

[#Hayek1945]: F.A. Hayek, *The Use of Knowledge in Society*, 35 Am. Econ. Rev. 519 (1945) (arguing that the central economic problem is the coordination of dispersed knowledge, which markets solve better than central planning).

[#Knight1921]: Frank H. Knight, *Risk, Uncertainty and Profit* (1921) (distinguishing between "risk" which is insurable/calculable, and "uncertainty" which is not; profit is the reward for bearing uncertainty).

[#Olson1965]: Mancur Olson, *The Logic of Collective Action: Public Goods and the Theory of Groups* (1965) (explaining why concentrated interests organize more effectively than diffuse interests due to free-rider problems).

[#Schumpeter1942]: Joseph A. Schumpeter, *Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy* (1942) (introducing "creative destruction" as the essential fact about capitalism; entrepreneurs disrupt static equilibrium).

[#Williamson1985]: Oliver E. Williamson, The Economic Institutions of Capitalism: Firms, Markets, Relational Contracting (New York: Free Press, 1985) (developing transaction cost economics theory and explaining why contracts cannot specify all contingencies, requiring governance mechanisms like fiduciary duties to fill contractual gaps).

[#Williamson2002]: Oliver E. Williamson, *The Theory of the Firm as Governance Structure: From Choice to Contract*, 16 J. Econ. Persp. 171 (2002) (summarizing transaction cost economics and the firm as a governance structure for managing relational contracts).

[#Kirzner1973]: Israel M. Kirzner, *Competition and Entrepreneurship* (Univ. of Chi. Press 1973) (foundational theory of entrepreneurial alertness; the entrepreneur is not merely a calculator optimizing given information but a discoverer who spots gaps in the market, inefficiencies in existing arrangements, and profit opportunities that others overlook; this alertness drives innovation and resource reallocation but also creates informational advantages that can be exploited) (*"Competition and Entrepreneurship"*).

[#DelawareDivisionCorps]: Delaware Division of Corporations, [*About the Division of Corporations*](https://perma.cc/59NS-JH9W) (last visted on Jan. 21, 2026) (reporting that more than 2,000,000 business entities, and more than 66% of the Fortune 500, have chosen Delaware as their legal home).

// NEW CITES 2026-01-21

[#Summers]: Summers v. Dooley, 94 Idaho 87, 481 P.2d 318 (Idaho 1971) (holding that in a two-person partnership with equal management rights, one partner cannot unilaterally hire an employee over the other partner's objection and charge the partnership for the expense; partnership statute requiring majority vote for ordinary business decisions means that with a 50-50 split, disagreement results in maintenance of the status quo) (*”Summer”*).

// === REVISED UNIFORM PARTNERSHIP ACT (RUPA) - ADDITIONAL PROVISIONS ===

[#RUPA301(a)]: Unif. P'ship Act § 301(a) (1997) (Nat'l Conf. Comm'rs on Unif. State L.) (each partner is an agent of the partnership for the purpose of its business; an act of a partner, including the execution of an instrument in the partnership name, for apparently carrying on in the ordinary course the partnership business or business of the kind carried on by the partnership binds the partnership, unless the partner had no authority to act for the partnership in the particular matter and the person with whom the partner was dealing knew or had received a notification that the partner lacked authority).

[#RUPA301(b)]: Unif. P'ship Act § 301(b) (1997) (Nat'l Conf. Comm'rs on Unif. State L.) (limiting partner's apparent authority; an act of a partner which is not apparently for carrying on in the ordinary course the partnership business or business of the kind carried on by the partnership does not bind the partnership unless authorized by the other partners).

[#RUPA403(c)]: Unif. P'ship Act § 403(c) (1997) (Nat'l Conf. Comm'rs on Unif. State L.) (each partner and the partnership shall furnish to a partner, and to the legal representative of a deceased partner or partner under legal disability, information concerning the partnership's business and affairs reasonably required for the proper exercise of the partner's rights and duties under the partnership agreement or this Act).

[#RUPA404(d)]: Unif. P'ship Act § 404(d) (1997) (Nat'l Conf. Comm'rs on Unif. State L.) (a partner shall discharge the duties to the partnership and the other partners under this Act or under the partnership agreement and exercise any rights consistently with the obligation of good faith and fair dealing).

[#RUPA801(a)]: Unif. P'ship Act § 801(a) (1997) (Nat'l Conf. Comm'rs on Unif. State L.) (a partnership is dissolved, and its business must be wound up, upon the occurrence of specified events, including in a partnership at will, the partnership's having notice from a partner of that partner's express will to withdraw as a partner, or on a later date specified by the partner).

[#Martin]: Martin v. Peyton, 158 N.E. 77 (N.Y. 1928) (holding that lenders who received profit-sharing rights and certain oversight privileges did not become partners merely by virtue of those arrangements; agreements granting lenders rights to inspect books and veto speculative transactions were reasonable measures to protect their investment rather than evidence of partnership formation; the intent clearly expressed in unambiguous contract language controls over outward appearances) (*”Martin”*).

[#Henderson2009]: M. Todd Henderson, *The Story of Dodge v. Ford Motor Company: Everything Old Is New Again*, in Corporate Law Stories 37 (J. Mark Ramseyer ed., 2009) (arguing that Ford’s "eleemosynary" testimony was likely a strategic error in a deposition intended to cover up a predatory business tactic against the Dodge brothers) (“*Story of Dodge*”).

[#BerleMeans1932]: Adolf A. Berle & Gardiner C. Means, *The Modern Corporation and Private Property* (1932) (identifying the separation of ownership and control as the fundamental structural characteristic of the modern economy).

[#BankAugusta]: *Bank of Augusta v. Earle*, 38 U.S. (13 Pet.) 519 (1839) (holding that a corporation created by one state has no legal existence outside that state's boundaries, but can operate in other states through the principle of comity) (“*Bank of Augusta*”).

[#ElfAtochem]: Elf Atochem N. Am., Inc. v. Jaffari, 727 A.2d 286 (Del. 1999) (holding that the operating agreement is binding on the LLC and its members, even if the LLC itself did not sign it, and enforcing a mandatory arbitration clause) (“*Elf*”).

[#QueenofAngels]: *Queen of Angels Hosp. v. Younger*, 66 Cal. App. 3d 359 (Ct. App. 1977) (holding that nonprofit hospital directors violated duty of obedience by selling hospital assets and distributing proceeds rather than using them for charitable healthcare purposes) (“*Queen of Angels*”).

Law Inst. 2011) (providing for disgorgement of profits from opportunistic breach of contract and breach of fiduciary duty to prevent unjust enrichment and deter wrongful conduct by making breach categorically unprofitable).

[#eBay]: *eBay Domestic Holdings, Inc. v. Newmark*, 16 A.3d 1 (Del. Ch. 2010) (holding that directors of for-profit corporation breached fiduciary duties by adopting poison pill and rights plan to protect corporate culture rather than maximizing stockholder value, stating directors "are bound by the fiduciary duties and standards that accompany [the for-profit] form" including "acting to promote the value of the corporation for the benefit of its stockholders").

[#NVCA_COI]: National Venture Capital Association, [Model Amended and Restated Certificate of Incorporation](https://perma.cc/T38X-PHZR) (updated October 2025) (standard-form certificate of incorporation for venture-backed corporations).

[#NVCA_PSPA]: National Venture Capital Association, [Model Preferred Stock Purchase Agreement](https://perma.cc/6NTK-9WAZ) (updated October 2025) (standard-form stock purchase agreement for venture-capital investors).

[#NVCA_IRA]: National Venture Capital Association, [Model Investors’ Rights Agreement](https://perma.cc/45HC-RT3L) (updated October 2025) (standard-form stock investors’ rights agreement for venture-capital investors).

[#NVCA_VA]: National Venture Capital Association, [Model Voting Agreement](https://perma.cc/U2BK-A2JB) (updated October 2025) (standard-form voting agreement for venture-capital investors).

[#Marsh]: Harold Marsh, Jr., *Are Directors Trustees? Conflict of Interest and Corporate Morality*, 22 Bus. Law. 35, 36-39 (1966) (describing the common law rule that interested director transactions were voidable at the corporation's election regardless of fairness).

// New citations 2026-01-23 For chapter 9

[#Tornetta]: *Tornetta v. Musk*, [310 A.3d 430](https://perma.cc/TD7Q-GWF9) (Del. Ch. Jan. 30, 2024) (holding that Elon Musk was a controlling stockholder of Tesla despite owning only 21.9% of the stock based on his status as "superstar CEO"; voiding $55.8 billion pay package under entire fairness standard because neither the compensation committee approval nor stockholder vote constituted adequate cleansing).

[#Citigroup]: *In re Citigroup Inc. S'holder Deriv. Litig.*, [964 A.2d 106](https://www.casemine.com/judgement/us/5914b1f7add7b0493475cce3) (Del. Ch. 2009) (dismissing *Caremark* oversight claims against Citigroup directors for failing to implement board-level risk management systems, holding that allegations of inadequate monitoring did not overcome the business judgment rule's presumption of good faith).

[#SB21]: [Del. S.B. 21](https://perma.cc/N682-EPLS) (enacted Mar. 25, 2025) (amending Del. Code Ann. tit. 8, §§ 144, 220 to codify a bright-line definition of "controlling stockholder," establish an "either/or" cleansing standard for controller transactions, and narrow Section 220 inspection rights regarding informal communications).

[#TeslaReincorp]: Tesla, Inc., [Definitive Proxy Statement on Schedule 14A](https://perma.cc/EAV7-22LK) (filed May 28, 2024) (disclosing shareholder vote on redomestication from Delaware to Texas; arguing that Delaware case law had become "indeterminate" and that Texas offered greater stability).

[#TeslaTexasStandard]: Sean Saldana, [*Tesla Reincorporates in Texas After Shareholder Approval*](https://perma.cc/8QZZ-DFTW), Texas Standard (June 20, 2024) (confirming Tesla's filing with the Texas Secretary of State following shareholder approval).

[#SpaceXReincorp]: [*Musk Says SpaceX Has Moved Its Incorporation to Texas from Delaware*](https://finance.yahoo.com/news/musk-says-spacex-moved-incorporation-004904752.html), Reuters (Feb. 14, 2024) (reporting SpaceX's transition to Texas and Musk's public advice for companies to leave Delaware).

[#NeuralinkReincorp]: *Musk's Neuralink Switches Location of Incorporation to Nevada*](https://www.reuters.com/business/musks-neuralink-switches-location-incorporation-bloomberg-news-2024-02-09/), Reuters (Feb. 9, 2024) (reporting Neuralink's relocation to Nevada immediately following the *Tornetta* decision).

[#GlassLewis2025]: [*The State of US Reincorporation in 2025: A Growing Threat or Reality? Examining DEXIT*] (https://perma.cc/928N-GX4B), Glass Lewis (Oct. 19, 2025) (documenting that 64.3% of companies proposing reincorporation in 2025 sought to leave Delaware, with a majority being controlled companies).

[#Bainbridge2025]: Stephen M. Bainbridge, *DExit Drivers: Is Delaware's Dominance Threatened?*, [50:4 Iowa J. Corp. L. 823](https://perma.cc/WA44-SRKW) (2025) (analyzing the empirical data of firms reincorporating from 2012–2024 and advising scrutiny of liability protections in "DExit" disclosures).

[#Ellis2022]: Sam Ellis, [*Musk: Feared or Loved? Delaware Chancery Court’s Controlling Shareholder Doctrine and the Expansion of Judicial Inquiry into Directorial Decision Making*](https://perma.cc/59NQ-KCVJ), U. Chi. Bus. L. Rev. (2022) (analyzing the *Tornetta* decision and its impact on judicial deference to board decisions).

[#Romano1993]: Roberta Romano, [*The Genius of American Corporate Law*](https://www.aei.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/07/-the-genius-of-american-corporate-law_162946985222.pdf) (AEI Press 1993) (arguing that Delaware's competitive advantage derives from the responsiveness of its legal system).

[#Romano1985]: Roberta Romano, *Law as a Product: Some Pieces of the Incorporation Puzzle*, 1 J.L. Econ. & Org. 225 (1985) (demonstrating that firms incorporate in Delaware for its specialized judiciary and institutional responsiveness).
[#Barzuza2012]: Michal Barzuza, [*Market Segmentation: The Rise of Nevada as a Liability Free Jurisdiction*](https://perma.cc/BM3P-KMBT), 98 Virginia L. Rev. 935 (2013) (establishing "market segmentation" theory: Nevada competes by offering "liability-free zones" rather than replicating Delaware's expertise).

[#Barzuza2024]: Michal Barzuza, [*Nevada v. Delaware: The New Market for Corporate Law*](https://dx.doi.org/10.2139/ssrn.4746878), European Corporate Governance Institute Working Paper No. 677/2251 (July 2024) (arguing that Nevada’s highly protective corporate law regime is emerging as a serious competitive threat to Delaware by sharply weakening shareholder litigation rights and fiduciary constraints, thereby creating pressure for a downward “snowballing” race in American corporate law).

[#KahanRock2025]: Marcel Kahan & Edward Rock, [*The New Political Economy of Delaware Corporate Lawmaking*](https://perma.cc/78YM-384E), Harv. L. Sch. F. on Corp. Governance (Oct. 6, 2025) (arguing that recent politically driven DGCL amendments responding to controller and client pressure have broken Delaware’s traditional technocratic lawmaking equilibrium, threatening its corporate law hegemony and requiring institutional reform via a more independent, representative Corporation Law Commission).

[#Bainbridge2025]: Stephen M. Bainbridge, [*DExit Drivers: Is Delaware's Dominance Threatened?*](https://perma.cc/Z6QW-PCCU), 50 Iowa J. Corp. L. 823 (2025) (analyzing empirical data of firms reincorporating from 2012–2024; finding network effects remain strong but acknowledging "prestige leak" among controlled companies).

[#Verstein2026]: Andrew Verstein, [*An Update on DExit, from the Corporate Census*](https://perma.cc/HH5Z-QLC8), Harv. L. Sch. F. on Corp. Governance (Jan. 15, 2026) (reporting Delaware experienced net 30% increase in new formations in 2025 despite DExit phenomenon).

[#MatchGroup]: *In re Match Group, Inc. Derivative Litig.*, [315 A.3d 446](https://perma.cc/G4CT-PSH8) (Del. 2024) (holding that controller transactions require both independent special committee approval and majority-of-minority stockholder vote to shift from entire fairness to business judgment review).

[#Palkon]: *Palkon v. Maffei*, [311 A.3d 255](https://perma.cc/T5L2-AY4L) (Del. Ch. 2024) (refusing to dismiss claims that TripAdvisor's reincorporation to Nevada was self-dealing transaction to insulate controller from fiduciary liability).

[#AllisChalmers]: *Graham v. Allis-Chalmers Mfg. Co.*, [188 A.2d 125](https://law.justia.com/cases/delaware/supreme-court/1963/188-a-2d-125-3.html) (Del. 1963) (holding that directors have no duty to install a system of corporate espionage to ferret out wrongdoing and may rely on subordinates absent "red flags" or cause for suspicion; the historical standard prior to *Caremark*).

[#AllisChalmers]: *Graham v. Allis-Chalmers Mfg. Co.*, 188 A.2d 125 (Del. 1963) (holding that directors have no duty to install a system of corporate espionage to ferret out wrongdoing and may rely on subordinates absent "red flags" or cause for suspicion).

[#USG1991]: [U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual 1991](https://perma.cc/4QCZ-JLPC) § 8A1.2 (creating incentives for organizations to maintain effective compliance programs to detect and prevent criminal conduct).

[#USG2025]: [U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual 2025](https://perma.cc/YK7B-NW5S) § 8B2.1 (continuing to incentivize effective compliance and ethics programs by offering culpability score reductions for organizations that prevent and detect criminal conduct).

[#Auriga]: *Auriga Capital Corp. v. Gatz Props., LLC*, 40 A.3d 839 (Del. Ch. 2012) (enforcing an LLC operating agreement that eliminated traditional fiduciary duties, holding that sophisticated parties are free to contract out of fiduciary obligations).

[#Barzuza2024]: Michal Barzuza & David Webber, *Nevada vs. Delaware*, 98 N.Y.U. L. Rev. 101 (2023) (analyzing the "DExit" phenomenon and arguing that Nevada competes by offering laxer fiduciary standards for controlling stockholders).

[#Boeing]: *In re The Boeing Co. Deriv. Litig.*, No. 2019-0907-MTZ, 2021 WL 4059934 (Del. Ch. Sept. 7, 2021) (denying dismissal of *Caremark* claims where the board failed to establish a committee charged with monitoring airplane safety—a "mission critical" risk).

[#Kahn]: *Kahn v. M & F Worldwide Corp.*, 88 A.3d 635 (Del. 2014) (holding that the business judgment rule applies to controller buyouts conditioned *ab initio* on approval by a special committee and a majority-of-the-minority vote).

[#R3dRestitution39]: Restatement (Third) of Restitution and Unjust Enrichment § 39 (Am. L. Inst. 2011) (authorizing disgorgement of profits derived from an opportunistic breach of contract).

[#R3dRestitution43]: Restatement (Third) of Restitution and Unjust Enrichment § 43 (Am. L. Inst. 2011) (stating that a person who obtains a benefit in breach of a fiduciary duty is liable in restitution for the profit realized).

[#ALIPrinciples301]: American Law Institute, Principles of Corporate Governance: Analysis and Recommendations § 3.01 (1994).

[#SOX301]: Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002, Pub. L. No. 107-204, § 301, 116 Stat. 745, 775-77 (codified at 15 U.S.C. § 78j-1).

[#McDonalds]: *In re McDonald's Corp. S'holder Deriv. Litig.*, 289 A.3d 343 (Del. Ch. 2023) (holding that corporate officers owe the same fiduciary duty of oversight as directors, but that the scope of an officer’s oversight duty is context-dependent and limited to matters within the officer’s area of responsibility).

[#Gagliardi]: *Gagliardi v. TriFoods Int'l, Inc.*, 683 A.2d 1049 (Del. Ch. 1996) (explaining that the business judgment rule exists to prevent director risk-aversion and encourage the pursuit of high-return projects).

[#Nemec]: *Nemec v. Shrader*, 991 A.2d 1120 (Del. 2010) (clarifying that Delaware’s implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing is a limited, gap‑filling doctrine that cannot be used to re-trade or override express contractual rights).

[#Ribstein]: Larry E. Ribstein, *The Uncorporation and Corporate Indeterminacy*, 2009 U. Ill. L. Rev. 131 (contrasting corporate mandatory rules with partnership/LLC contractual freedom).

[#RUPA103b]: Revised Uniform Partnership Act § 103(b) (1997) (listing non-waivable provisions, including the prohibition against eliminating the duty of loyalty or unreasonably reducing the duty of care).

[#RUPA404b]: Revised Uniform Partnership Act § 404(b) (1997) (defining the duty of loyalty as limited to accounting to the partnership, refraining from dealing as an adverse party, and refraining from competing).

[#RUPA404c]: Revised Uniform Partnership Act § 404(c) (1997) (defining the duty of care as refraining from engaging in grossly negligent or reckless conduct, intentional misconduct, or a knowing violation of law).

[#RMNCA830]: Model Nonprofit Corp. Act § 8.30 (3d ed. 2008) (establishing the general standards of conduct for directors, requiring them to act in good faith, with the care an ordinarily prudent person in a like position would exercise, and in a manner reasonably believed to be in the best interests of the corporation).

[#RMNCA830cmt]: Model Nonprofit Corp. Act § 8.30 cmt. (3d ed. 2008) (clarifying that the statutory standard of conduct focuses on the process of decision-making rather than the outcome, and differentiating this standard from the judicial business judgment rule).

[#ULPA110]: Unif. Ltd. P'ship Act § 110 (2001) (enumerating the non-waivable provisions of a limited partnership agreement, including the requirements that the agreement may not eliminate the duty of loyalty, unreasonably reduce the duty of care, or eliminate the obligation of good faith and fair dealing).

[#RMNCA830]: Revised Model Nonprofit Corp. Act § 8.30 (2008).

[#OberlyKirby]: Oberly v. Kirby, 592 A.2d 445, 462 (Del. 1991) (holding that directors of nonprofit corporations owe duties of care and loyalty, and "must discharge their duties in good faith with a view to the purposes and interests of the corporation").

[#Coffee1986]: John C. Coffee, Jr., *Understanding the Plaintiff's Attorney: The Implications of Economic Theory for Private Enforcement of Law Through Class and Derivative Actions*, 86 Colum. L. Rev. 669 (1986) (aruging that plaintiff’s attorneys in class and derivative actions behave as entrepreneurial “private attorneys general” whose incentives are systematically misaligned with those of dispersed shareholders, producing underinvestment in meritorious suits, structural collusion, and premature, low-value settlements, and thus calls for redesigning fee and procedural rules to reduce agency costs rather than to curtail private enforcement itself).

[#SugarlandIndustries]: *Sugarland Indus., Inc. v. Thomas*, 420 A.2d 142 (Del. 1980) (articulating that in awarding attorneys’ fees in Delaware representative actions, the primary factor is the benefit achieved for the corporation and its stockholders, with other factors playing a secondary, supporting role).

[#DELLC18-1101]: Del. Code Ann. tit. 6, § 18-1101 (declaring the policy of the Act to give maximum effect to the principle of freedom of contract and the enforceability of limited liability company agreements).

[#MONYGroup]: *In re MONY Group Inc. S'holders Litig.*, 853 A.2d 661 (Del. Ch. 2004) (applying *Schnell*[#Schnell] and holding that a disinterested board may postpone a merger vote, set a new record date, and reuse existing proxies under the business judgment rule so long as it does not coerce or mislead stockholders and provides adequate corrective disclosure) (“*MONY Group*”).

[#Aprahamian]: Aprahamian v. HBO & Co., 531 A.2d 1204 (Del. Ch. 1987) (applying *Schnell*[#Schnell] and enjoining incumbent directors from postponing a scheduled annual meeting once a proxy contest was underway, holding that interested incumbents bear the burden to justify any delay and that election machinery cannot be manipulated to frustrate stockholder choice) (“*Aprahamian*”).

[#Stahl]: *Stahl v. Apple Bancorp, Inc.*, 579 A.2d 1115 (Del. Ch. 1990) (applying *Schnell*[#Schnell] and upholding directors’ decision to withdraw a record date and defer the annual meeting in the face of a hostile tender offer and proxy contest, where the board reasonably viewed the bid as a threat and needed time to explore higher-value alternatives) (“*Stahl*”).

[#Openwave]: *In re Openwave Sys. Inc. S'holders Litig.*, 924 A.2d 228 (Del. Ch. 2007) (enforcing an advance notice bylaw and upholding the incumbents’ election where the hedge fund missed the nomination deadline, rejecting “confusing bylaws” and “good governance” arguments, and declining to find disenfranchisement or a disclosure violation) (“*Openwave*”).

[#Williams]: *Williams v. Geier*, 671 A.2d 1368 (Del. 1996) (applying *Schnell*[#Schnell] and upholding a recapitalization that gave long‑term holders ten‑for‑one voting rights over short‑term holders, applying the business judgment rule where a disinterested board proposed the charter amendment and a fully informed stockholder majority approved it) (“*Williams*”).

[#CSX]: *CSX Corp. v. Child.’s Inv. Fund Mgmt. (UK) LLP*, 654 F.3d 276 (2d Cir. 2011), aff’g in relevant part 562 F. Supp. 2d 511 (S.D.N.Y. 2008) (holding that hedge funds that accumulated a large stake through total‑return swaps and coordinated share purchases violated Schedule 13D disclosure requirements but that “sterilizing” their voting rights was inappropriate once shareholders had received the required information in time to cast an informed vote) (“*CSX*”).

[#Hu2006]: Henry T.C. Hu & Bernard Black, [*The New Vote Buying: Empty Voting and Hidden (Morphable) Ownership*](https://perma.cc/5N3M-U3LR), 79 S. Cal. L. Rev. 811 (2006) (documenting the empty voting phenomenon where investors decouple voting rights from economic ownership).

[#Hu2008]: Henry T.C. Hu & Bernard Black, *Equity and Debt Decoupling and Empty Voting II: Importance and Extensions*, 156 U. Pa. L. Rev. 625 (2008) (extending empty voting analysis to debt decoupling).

[#Broadridge2024]: Broadridge Financial Solutions, *Proxy Services* (last visited January 24, 2026) (describing the electronic proxy processing infrastructure for U.S. public companies).

[#Bebchuk2005]: Lucian Arye Bebchuk, *The Case for Increasing Shareholder Power*, 118 Harv. L. Rev. 833 (2005) (arguing for mandatory declassification of boards to increase accountability).

[#Bebchuk2007]: Lucian A. Bebchuk, *The Myth of the Shareholder Franchise*, 93 Va. L. Rev. 675 (2007) (documenting the "holdover director" problem under plurality voting and advocating for a majority voting standard).

[#Mirvis2007]: Theodore N. Mirvis, Paul K. Rowe & William Savitt, [*Bebchuk’s “Case for Increasing Shareholder Power”: An Opposition*](https://perma.cc/9KQX-SYTP), Harv. L. Rev. Forum (May 30, 2007) (arguing that Bebchuk’s proposal for increased shareholder power would destabilize the historically successful Delaware director-centric governance regime and exacerbate practical and doctrinal problems in corporate law).

[#Kastiel2021]: Kobi Kastiel, *Lucian Bebchuk and the Study of Corporate Governance*, 88 U. Chi. L. Rev. 1689 (“surveying and synthesizing Bebchuk’s major contributions to corporate governance theory, empirical research, and policy influence”).

// New cites 2024-01-25 for Chapter 10 on going public

[#GoogleFounders2004]: Larry Page & Sergey Brin, [*2004 Founders' IPO Letter*](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1288776/000119312512160666/d333341dex993.htm), SEC Form S-1 Registration Statement (Apr. 29, 2004) (explaining Google's decision to implement dual-class voting structure at IPO to preserve founder control over long-term decision-making and stating "we are not a conventional company").

[#GoogleDutchIPO]: C.N.V. Krishnan et al., [*Going Dutch: The Google IPO*](https://lawcat.berkeley.edu/record/1119854/files/fulltext.pdf), Berkeley Law (2004) (analyzing Google's unconventional use of Dutch auction mechanism for its 2004 IPO and discussing implications for underwriter role and price discovery).

[#FacebookIPO]: [*Initial Public Offering of Facebook*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Initial_public_offering_of_Facebook), Wikipedia (documenting Facebook's May 2012 IPO process, dual-class structure giving Mark Zuckerberg 57% voting control with only 13% equity ownership, technical failures on opening day, and resulting shareholder litigation).

[#ZuckerbergControl]: [*How Mark Zuckerberg Secured Voting Control at Meta*](https://www.linkedin.com/posts/guillermoflor_mark-zuckerberg-only-owns-13-of-metas-stock-activity-7385745533157437440-09WI), LinkedIn (Oct. 18, 2025) (explaining mechanics of Facebook's dual-class structure and irrevocable voting proxies from early employees and investors that give Zuckerberg majority voting control).

[#AlibabaSynthetic]: Jesse M. Fried & Ehud Kamar, [*Alibaba: A Case Study of Synthetic Control*](https://corpgov.law.harvard.edu/2020/08/11/alibaba-a-case-study-of-synthetic-control/), Harv. L. Sch. F. on Corp. Governance (Aug. 10, 2020) (analyzing how Jack Ma maintains control of Alibaba despite owning less than 5% equity through partnership nomination structures, VIE arrangements, and dominance over Ant Group which employs key partnership members).

[#DualClassEmory]: David J. Berger, [*The Dual-Class Share Structure*](https://scholarlycommons.law.emory.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1016&context=ecgar), Emory Corp. Governance & Accountability Rev. (2015) (examining history and governance implications of dual-class voting structures in public companies, including arguments for and against permitting founder control mechanisms at IPO).

[#AdvanceNoticeDE]: [*Delaware Chancery Court Holds That Advance Notice Provisions Must Clearly State Requirements*](https://www.corporatesecuritieslawblog.com/2008/07/delaware-chancery-court-holds-that-advance-notice-provisions-must-clearly-and), Corp. Sec. L. Blog (Mar. 30, 2021) (discussing Delaware case law requiring that advance notice bylaws clearly and unambiguously specify nomination deadlines and information requirements, with ambiguities construed in favor of shareholder participation).

[#ProxyAccessDE]: Jill E. Fisch, [*Delaware's New Proxy Access: Much Ado About Nothing?*](https://scholarship.law.upenn.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=4238&context=faculty_scholarship), U. Penn. L. Scholarship (analyzing Delaware's adoption of proxy access provisions allowing shareholders to include director nominees in company proxy materials and questioning whether practical barriers limit effectiveness).

[#DECancellation]: [*Cancellation of Special Stockholders' Meeting Improper*](https://www.delawarelitigation.com/2007/02/articles/chancery-court-updates/cancellation-of-special-stockholders-meeting-improper), Del. Litig. Blog (Feb. 19, 2007) (summarizing Court of Chancery decision applying Schnell doctrine to enjoin board's cancellation of special stockholder meeting after dissidents called meeting to challenge board composition).

[#AdvanceNoticeBalance]: [*Advance Notice Bylaws and Proxy Contests: A Balancing Act*](https://www.faegredrinker.com/en/insights/publications/2024/10/advance-notice-bylaws-and-proxy-contests-a-balancing-act), Faegre Drinker (Jul. 10, 2025) (examining tension between board's legitimate interest in orderly nomination process and shareholders' franchise rights, discussing when advance notice requirements become impermissible under Schnell).

[#SecuritiesAct1933_5]: 15 U.S.C. § 77e (requiring registration of non-exempt securities with the SEC before public offering).

[#SecuritiesAct1933_7]: 15 U.S.C. § 77g (establishing comprehensive disclosure requirements for registration statements).

[#SecuritiesAct1933_8]: 15 U.S.C. § 77h (establishing 20-day effective period for registration statements and SEC authority to delay effectiveness).

[#ExchangeAct1934_13a]: 15 U.S.C. § 78m(a) (requiring Form 10-K annual reports within 60-90 days of fiscal year end and Form 10-Q quarterly reports within 40-45 days of quarter end).

[#ExchangeAct1934_8K]: 15 U.S.C. § 78m(a) (authorizing SEC to require Current Reports on Form 8-K to disclose material events within four business days of occurrence).

[#ExchangeAct1934_14a]: 15 U.S.C. § 78n(a) (requiring SEC filing and shareholder disclosure of proxy materials for solicitations).

[#ExchangeAct1934_12g]: 15 U.S.C. § 78l(g) (establishing registration threshold for companies with more than 2,000 persons of record and $10 million in assets).

[#SarbanesOxley2002_302]: 15 U.S.C. § 7241 (requiring CEO and CFO certification of accuracy of financial reports and imposing criminal penalties for false certification).

[#SarbanesOxley2002_404]: 15 U.S.C. § 7262 (requiring public companies to establish and maintain internal controls over financial reporting and external auditor attestation to management's assessment).

[#JOBSAct2012_Title5]: Jumpstart Our Business Startups (JOBS) Act, Pub. L. No. 112-106, Title V, 126 Stat. 306 (2012) (raising Securities Exchange Act Section 12(g) registration threshold from 500 shareholders to 2,000 persons).

[#SOXCompliance_AuditBoard]: AuditBoard, *What is SOX Compliance? Complete Guide* (providing overview of Section 302 and Section 404 requirements and noting average annual compliance costs of $1-2M and 10,000 hours per year).

[#SOXCosts_RadicalCompliance]: Radical Compliance, *A Question on SOX Compliance Costs* (citing GAO report on compliance costs with particular burden spike at Section 404(b) threshold).
